<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Xenon</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the chemical element. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Xenon_(disambiguation)" title="Xenon (disambiguation)">Xenon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 54</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Xenon,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">54</span></sub>Xe</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg"><img alt="Xenon discharge tube.jpg" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3008" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a><div>A xenon-filled <a href="/wiki/Gas-filled_tube" title="Gas-filled tube">discharge</a> tube glowing light blue</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#c0ffff">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<div class="plainlist" style="margin: 0; text-align: left;"><ul><li style="margin: 0; text-indent: -1em; padding-left: 1em;"><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">ZEN</span>-on</i></a>)</li><li style="margin: 0; text-indent: -1em; padding-left: 1em;"><span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">ZEE</span>-non</i></a>)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
colorless gas, exhibiting a blue glow when placed in an electric field</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r865336399">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_xenon" title="Template:Infobox xenon"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_xenon" title="Template talk:Infobox xenon"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_xenon&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Xenon</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Xe</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 54. It is a colorless, dense, odorless <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> found in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> in trace amounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Although generally unreactive, xenon can undergo a few <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a> such as the formation of <a href="/wiki/Xenon_hexafluoroplatinate" title="Xenon hexafluoroplatinate">xenon hexafluoroplatinate</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas_compound" title="Noble gas compound">noble gas compound</a> to be synthesized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beautiful_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-beautiful-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon is used in <a href="/wiki/Flashtube#Xenon" title="Flashtube">flash lamps</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burke_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-burke-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Xenon_arc_lamp" title="Xenon arc lamp">arc lamps</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mellor_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-15">[15]</a></sup> and as a <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthesia" title="General anaesthesia">general anesthetic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanders_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanders-16">[16]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Excimer_laser" title="Excimer laser">excimer laser</a> design used a xenon <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a> molecule (Xe<sub>2</sub>) as the <a href="/wiki/Active_laser_medium" title="Active laser medium">lasing medium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basov_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-basov-17">[17]</a></sup> and the earliest <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> designs used xenon flash lamps as <a href="/wiki/Laser_pumping" title="Laser pumping">pumps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toyserkani_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-toyserkani-18">[18]</a></sup> Xenon is used to search for hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">weakly interacting massive particles</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball-19">[19]</a></sup> and as the <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a> for <a href="/wiki/Ion_thruster" title="Ion thruster">ion thrusters</a> in spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saccoccia_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-saccoccia-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Naturally occurring xenon consists of <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_xenon" title="Isotopes of xenon">eight stable isotopes</a>. More than 40 unstable xenon isotopes undergo <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>, and the isotope ratios of xenon are an important tool for studying the early history of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaneoka_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-kaneoka-21">[21]</a></sup> Radioactive <a href="/wiki/Xenon-135" title="Xenon-135">xenon-135</a> is produced by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> from <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_iodine#Iodine-135" title="Isotopes of iodine">iodine-135</a> (a product of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>), and is the most significant (and unwanted) <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron absorber</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stacey_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-stacey-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Xenon was discovered in England by the Scottish chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Ramsay" title="William Ramsay">William Ramsay</a> and English chemist <a href="/wiki/Morris_Travers" title="Morris Travers">Morris Travers</a> in September 1898,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-23">[23]</a></sup> shortly after their discovery of the elements <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>. They found xenon in the residue left over from evaporating components of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_air" title="Liquid air">liquid air</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Ramsay suggested the name <i>xenon</i> for this gas from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word <i>ξένον</i> [xenon], neuter singular form of <i>ξένος</i> [xenos], meaning 'foreign(er)', 'strange(r)', or 'guest'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1902, Ramsay estimated the proportion of xenon in the Earth's atmosphere to be one part in 20 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1930s, American engineer <a href="/wiki/Harold_Eugene_Edgerton" title="Harold Eugene Edgerton">Harold Edgerton</a> began exploring <a href="/wiki/Strobe_light" title="Strobe light">strobe light</a> technology for <a href="/wiki/High-speed_photography" title="High-speed photography">high speed photography</a>. This led him to the invention of the xenon <a href="/wiki/Flashtube" title="Flashtube">flash lamp</a> in which light is generated by passing brief electric current through a tube filled with xenon gas. In 1934, Edgerton was able to generate flashes as brief as one <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">microsecond</a> with this method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burke_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-burke-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1939, American physician <a href="/wiki/Albert_R._Behnke" title="Albert R. Behnke">Albert R. Behnke</a> Jr. began exploring the causes of "drunkenness" in deep-sea divers. He tested the effects of varying the breathing mixtures on his subjects, and discovered that this caused the divers to perceive a change in depth. From his results, he deduced that xenon gas could serve as an <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthetic</a>. Although Russian toxicologist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nikolay_Lazarev&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nikolay Lazarev (page does not exist)">Nikolay V. Lazarev</a> apparently studied xenon anesthesia in 1941, the first published report confirming xenon anesthesia was in 1946 by American medical researcher John H. Lawrence, who experimented on mice. Xenon was first used as a surgical anesthetic in 1951 by American anesthesiologist Stuart C. Cullen, who successfully used it with two patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1798" data-file-width="1856" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG/220px-An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG/330px-An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG/440px-An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_acrylic_cube_specially_prepared_for_element_collectors_containing_an_ampoule_filled_with_liquefied_xenon.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An acrylic cube specially prepared for element collectors containing liquefied xenon</div></div></div>
<p>Xenon and the other noble gases were for a long time considered to be completely chemically inert and not able to form <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>. However, while teaching at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_British_Columbia" title="University of British Columbia">University of British Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Bartlett_(chemist)" title="Neil Bartlett (chemist)">Neil Bartlett</a> discovered that the gas <a href="/wiki/Platinum_hexafluoride" title="Platinum hexafluoride">platinum hexafluoride</a> (PtF<sub>6</sub>) was a powerful <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">oxidizing</a> agent that could oxidize oxygen gas (O<sub>2</sub>) to form <a href="/wiki/Dioxygenyl_hexafluoroplatinate" title="Dioxygenyl hexafluoroplatinate">dioxygenyl hexafluoroplatinate</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>[PtF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Since O<sub>2</sub> and xenon have almost the same first <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization potential</a>, Bartlett realized that platinum hexafluoride might also be able to oxidize xenon. On March 23, 1962, he mixed the two gases and produced the first known compound of a noble gas, <a href="/wiki/Xenon_hexafluoroplatinate" title="Xenon hexafluoroplatinate">xenon hexafluoroplatinate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bartlettxe_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-bartlettxe-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beautiful_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-beautiful-13">[13]</a></sup> Bartlett thought its composition to be Xe<sup>+</sup>[PtF<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup>, but later work revealed that it was probably a mixture of various xenon-containing salts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grahm_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-grahm-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Since then, many other xenon compounds have been discovered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> in addition to some compounds of the noble gases <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Argon_fluorohydride" title="Argon fluorohydride">argon fluorohydride</a> (HArF),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Krypton_difluoride" title="Krypton difluoride">krypton difluoride</a> (KrF<sub>2</sub>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Radon_difluoride" title="Radon difluoride">radon fluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> By 1971, more than 80 xenon compounds were known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 1989, <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> scientists demonstrated a technology capable of manipulating individual <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>. The program, called <a href="/wiki/IBM_(atoms)" title="IBM (atoms)">IBM in atoms</a>, used a <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscope" title="Scanning tunneling microscope">scanning tunneling microscope</a> to arrange 35 individual xenon atoms on a substrate of chilled crystal of <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> to spell out the three letter company initialism. It was the first time atoms had been precisely positioned on a flat surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="812" data-file-width="957" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg/220px-Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg/330px-Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg/440px-Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xe_nanoparticles_in_Al.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Liquid (featureless) and crystalline solid Xe nanoparticles produced by implanting Xe<sup>+</sup> ions into aluminium at room temperature.</div></div></div>
<p>Xenon has <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 54; that is, its nucleus contains 54 <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>. At <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, pure xenon gas has a density of 5.761 kg/m<sup>3</sup>, about 4.5 times the density of the Earth's atmosphere at sea level, 1.217 kg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> As a liquid, xenon has a density of up to 3.100 g/mL, with the density maximum occurring at the triple point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-detectors_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-detectors-46">[46]</a></sup> Liquid xenon has a high polarizability due to its large atomic volume, and thus is an excellent solvent. It can dissolve hydrocarbons, biological molecules, and even water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Under the same conditions, the density of solid xenon, 3.640 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, is greater than the average density of <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a>, 2.75 g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-detectors_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-detectors-46">[46]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">gigapascals</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, xenon forms a metallic phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Solid xenon changes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Face-centered_cubic" title="Face-centered cubic">face-centered cubic</a> (fcc) to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Close-packing_of_spheres" title="Close-packing of spheres">hexagonal close packed</a> (hcp) crystal phase under pressure and begins to turn metallic at about 140 GPa, with no noticeable volume change in the hcp phase. It is completely metallic at 155 GPa. When metallized, xenon appears sky blue because it absorbs red light and transmits other visible frequencies. Such behavior is unusual for a metal and is explained by the relatively small width of the electron bands in that state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:148px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon-flash.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="183" data-file-width="146" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/Xenon-flash.jpg" width="146"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenon_flash" title="Xenon flash">Xenon flash</a><br/>(<a href="/wiki/File:Xenon-flash.gif" title="File:Xenon-flash.gif">animated version</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Liquid or solid xenon nanoparticles can be formed at room temperature by implanting Xe<sup>+</sup> ions into a solid matrix. Many solids have lattice constants smaller than solid Xe. This results in compression of the implanted Xe to pressures that may be sufficient for its liquefaction or solidification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon is a member of the zero-<a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">valence</a> elements that are called <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble</a> or <a href="/wiki/Inert_gas" title="Inert gas">inert gases</a>. It is inert to most common chemical reactions (such as combustion, for example) because the outer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_shell" title="Valence shell">valence shell</a> contains eight electrons. This produces a stable, minimum energy configuration in which the outer electrons are tightly bound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a <a href="/wiki/Gas-filled_tube" title="Gas-filled tube">gas-filled tube</a>, xenon emits a <a href="/wiki/Blue" title="Blue">blue</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lavender_(color)" title="Lavender (color)">lavenderish</a> glow when excited by <a href="/wiki/Electric_arc" title="Electric arc">electrical discharge</a>. Xenon emits a band of <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">emission lines</a> that span the visual spectrum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> but the most intense lines occur in the region of blue light, producing the coloration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_production">Occurrence and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Occurrence and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Xenon is a <a href="/wiki/Trace_gas" title="Trace gas">trace gas</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>, occurring at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6992870000000000000♠</span>87<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>1 nL/L</span> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" title="Parts per billion">parts per billion</a>), or approximately 1 part per 11.5 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-55">[55]</a></sup> It is also found as a component of gases emitted from some <a href="/wiki/Mineral_spring" title="Mineral spring">mineral springs</a>.
</p><p>Xenon is obtained commercially as a by-product of the <a href="/wiki/Air_separation" title="Air separation">separation of air</a> into <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>. After this separation, generally performed by <a href="/wiki/Fractional_distillation" title="Fractional distillation">fractional distillation</a> in a double-column plant, the <a href="/wiki/Liquid_oxygen" title="Liquid oxygen">liquid oxygen</a> produced will contain small quantities of krypton and xenon. By additional fractional distillation, the liquid oxygen may be enriched to contain 0.1–0.2% of a krypton/xenon mixture, which is extracted either by absorption onto <a href="/wiki/Silica_gel" title="Silica gel">silica gel</a> or by distillation. Finally, the krypton/xenon mixture may be separated into <a href="/wiki/Krypton" title="Krypton">krypton</a> and xenon by further distillation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Worldwide production of xenon in 1998 was estimated at 5,000–7,000 m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-58">[58]</a></sup> Because of its scarcity, xenon is much more expensive than the lighter noble gases—approximate prices for the purchase of small quantities in Europe in 1999 were 10 <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">€</a>/L for xenon, 1 €/L for krypton, and 0.20 €/L for neon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-58">[58]</a></sup> while the much more plentiful argon costs less than a cent per liter.
</p><p>Within the Solar System, the <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a> fraction of xenon is <span class="nowrap">1.56 × 10<sup>−8</sup></span>, for an <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundance</a> of approximately one part in 630 thousand of the total mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Xenon is relatively rare in the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>'s atmosphere, on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>. The abundance of xenon in the atmosphere of planet <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> is unusually high, about 2.6 times that of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mahaffy_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-mahaffy-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This abundance remains unexplained, but may have been caused by an early and rapid buildup of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a>—small, subplanetary bodies—before the heating of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">presolar disk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> (Otherwise, xenon would not have been trapped in the planetesimal ices.) The problem of the low terrestrial xenon may be explained by <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonding</a> of xenon to oxygen within <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, reducing the outgassing of xenon into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the lower-mass noble gases, the normal <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> process inside a star does not form xenon. Elements more massive than <a href="/wiki/Iron-56" title="Iron-56">iron-56</a> consume energy through fusion, and the synthesis of xenon represents no energy gain for a star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Instead, xenon is formed during <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> explosions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heymann_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-heymann-65">[65]</a></sup> in classical <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">nova</a> explosions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> by the slow neutron-capture process (<a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> stars that have exhausted their core hydrogen and entered the <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">asymptotic giant branch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and from radioactive decay, for example by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct</a> <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">iodine-129</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caldwell_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-caldwell-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_xenon" title="Isotopes of xenon">Isotopes of xenon</a></div>
<p>Naturally occurring xenon is composed of eight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable</a> <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>. This is more than any other element except <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, which has ten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The isotopes <sup>124</sup>Xe and <sup>134</sup>Xe are predicted by theory to undergo <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double beta decay</a>, but this has never been observed so they are considered stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In addition, more than 40 unstable isotopes that have been studied. The longest lived of these isotopes is <sup>136</sup>Xe, which undergoes double beta decay with a half-life of <span class="nowrap">2.11 × 10<sup>21</sup> yr</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EXO_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-EXO-71">[71]</a></sup> <sup>129</sup>Xe is produced by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> of <sup>129</sup><a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">I</a>, which has a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 16 million years. <sup>131m</sup>Xe, <sup>133</sup>Xe, <sup>133m</sup>Xe, and <sup>135</sup>Xe are some of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fission</a> products of <sup>235</sup><a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">U</a> and <sup>239</sup><a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">Pu</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caldwell_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-caldwell-68">[68]</a></sup> and are used to detect and monitor nuclear explosions.
</p><p>Nuclei of two of the stable <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_xenon" title="Isotopes of xenon">isotopes of xenon</a>, <sup>129</sup>Xe and <sup>131</sup>Xe, have non-zero intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momenta</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">nuclear spins</a>, suitable for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a>). The nuclear spins can be aligned beyond ordinary polarization levels by means of circularly polarized light and <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a> vapor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Spin_polarization" title="Spin polarization">spin polarization</a> of xenon <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> can surpass 50% of its maximum possible value, greatly exceeding the thermal equilibrium value dictated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paramagnetic" title="Paramagnetic">paramagnetic</a> statistics (typically 0.001% of the maximum value at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>, even in the strongest <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnets</a>). Such non-equilibrium alignment of spins is a temporary condition, and is called <i><a href="/wiki/Hyperpolarization_(physics)" title="Hyperpolarization (physics)">hyperpolarization</a></i>. The process of hyperpolarizing the xenon is called <i>optical pumping</i> (although the process is different from <a href="/wiki/Optical_pumping" title="Optical pumping">pumping a laser</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because a <sup>129</sup>Xe nucleus has a <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> of 1/2, and therefore a zero <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrupole_moment" title="Quadrupole moment">quadrupole moment</a>, the <sup>129</sup>Xe nucleus does not experience any quadrupolar interactions during collisions with other atoms, and the hyperpolarization persists for long periods even after the engendering light and vapor have been removed. Spin polarization of <sup>129</sup>Xe can persist from several <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">seconds</a> for xenon atoms dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> to several hours in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_phase" title="Gas phase">gas phase</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and several days in deeply frozen solid xenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_xenon" title="Isotopes of xenon"><sup>131</sup>Xe</a> has a nuclear spin value of ​<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span> and a nonzero <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadrupole_moment" title="Quadrupole moment">quadrupole moment</a>, and has t<sub>1</sub> relaxation times in the <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">millisecond</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a> ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some radioactive isotopes of xenon (for example, <sup>133</sup>Xe and <sup>135</sup>Xe) are produced by <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> irradiation of fissionable material within <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lanl_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-lanl-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Xenon-135" title="Xenon-135"><sup>135</sup>Xe</a> is of considerable significance in the operation of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear fission reactors</a>. <sup>135</sup>Xe has a huge <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_cross-section" title="Neutron cross-section">cross section</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_neutron" title="Thermal neutron">thermal neutrons</a>, 2.6×10<sup>6</sup> <a href="/wiki/Barn_(unit)" title="Barn (unit)">barns</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stacey_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-stacey-22">[22]</a></sup> and operates as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_absorber" title="Neutron absorber">neutron absorber</a> or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_poison" title="Nuclear poison">poison</a>" that can slow or stop the chain reaction after a period of operation. This was discovered in the earliest nuclear reactors built by the American <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> for <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> production. However, the designers had made provisions in the design to increase the reactor's reactivity (the number of neutrons per fission that go on to fission other atoms of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
<sup>135</sup>Xe reactor poisoning was a major factor in the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster#Conditions_before_the_accident" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> A shutdown or decrease of power of a reactor can result in buildup of <sup>135</sup>Xe, with reactor operation going into a condition known as the <a href="/wiki/Iodine_pit" title="Iodine pit">iodine pit</a>.
</p><p>Under adverse conditions, relatively high concentrations of radioactive xenon isotopes may emanate from cracked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_rod" title="Fuel rod">fuel rods</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> or fissioning of uranium in <a href="/wiki/Water_cooling" title="Water cooling">cooling water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because xenon is a tracer for two parent isotopes, xenon isotope ratios in <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> are a powerful tool for studying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formation_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation of the Solar System">formation of the Solar System</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iodine%E2%80%93xenon_dating" title="Iodine–xenon dating">iodine–xenon method</a> of <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">dating</a> gives the time elapsed between <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> and the condensation of a solid object from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a>. In 1960, physicist <a href="/wiki/John_Reynolds_(physicist)" title="John Reynolds (physicist)">John H. Reynolds</a> discovered that certain <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> contained an isotopic anomaly in the form of an overabundance of xenon-129. He inferred that this was a <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay product</a> of radioactive <a href="/wiki/Iodine-129" title="Iodine-129">iodine-129</a>. This isotope is produced slowly by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_spallation" title="Cosmic ray spallation">cosmic ray spallation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>, but is produced in quantity only in supernova explosions. Because the half-life of <sup>129</sup>I is comparatively short on a cosmological time scale (16 million years), this demonstrated that only a short time had passed between the supernova and the time the meteorites had solidified and trapped the <sup>129</sup>I. These two events (supernova and solidification of gas cloud) were inferred to have happened during the early history of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, because the <sup>129</sup>I isotope was likely generated shortly before the Solar System was formed, seeding the solar gas cloud with isotopes from a second source. This supernova source may also have caused collapse of the solar gas cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a similar way, xenon isotopic ratios such as <sup>129</sup>Xe/<sup>130</sup>Xe and <sup>136</sup>Xe/<sup>130</sup>Xe are a powerful tool for understanding planetary differentiation and early outgassing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaneoka_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-kaneoka-21">[21]</a></sup> For example, the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">atmosphere of Mars</a> shows a xenon abundance similar to that of Earth (0.08 parts per million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>) but Mars shows a greater abundance of <sup>129</sup>Xe than the Earth or the Sun. Since this isotope is generated by radioactive decay, the result may indicate that Mars lost most of its primordial atmosphere, possibly within the first 100 million years after the planet was formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In another example, excess <sup>129</sup>Xe found in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> well gases from <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> is believed to be from the decay of <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>-derived gases from soon after Earth's formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caldwell_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-caldwell-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Xenon_compounds" title="Category:Xenon compounds">Category:Xenon compounds</a>.</div>
<p>After Neil Bartlett's discovery in 1962 that xenon can form chemical compounds, a large number of xenon compounds have been discovered and described. Almost all known xenon compounds contain the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronegative" title="Electronegative">electronegative</a> atoms fluorine or oxygen. The chemistry of xenon in each oxidation state is analogous to that of the neighboring element <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> in the immediately lower oxidation state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harding1_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-harding1-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Halides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="A model of planar chemical molecule with a blue center atom (Xe) symmetrically bonded to four peripheral atoms (fluorine)." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="942" data-file-width="1100" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png/220px-Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png/330px-Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png/440px-Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenon-tetrafluoride-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Xenon_tetrafluoride" title="Xenon tetrafluoride">Xenon tetrafluoride</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_tetrafluoride.JPG"><img alt="Many cubic transparent crystals in a petri dish." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="334" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_tetrafluoride.JPG/220px-Xenon_tetrafluoride.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_tetrafluoride.JPG/330px-Xenon_tetrafluoride.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Xenon_tetrafluoride.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_tetrafluoride.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>XeF<sub>4</sub> crystals, 1962</div></div></div>
<p>Three <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluorides</a> are known: <a href="/wiki/Xenon_difluoride" title="Xenon difluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenon_tetrafluoride" title="Xenon tetrafluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xenon_hexafluoride" title="Xenon hexafluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span></a>. XeF is theorized to be unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> These are the starting points for the synthesis of almost all xenon compounds.
</p><p>The solid, crystalline difluoride <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is formed when a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a> and xenon gases is exposed to ultraviolet light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The ultraviolet component of ordinary daylight is sufficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Long-term heating of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> at high temperatures under an <span class="chemf nowrap">NiF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> catalyst yields <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tramsek_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-tramsek-92">[92]</a></sup> Pyrolysis of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoride" title="Sodium fluoride">NaF</a> yields high-purity <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The xenon fluorides behave as both fluoride acceptors and fluoride donors, forming salts that contain such cations as <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">Xe</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span><span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, and anions such as <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>. The green, paramagnetic <span class="chemf nowrap">Xe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is formed by the reduction of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> by xenon gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harding1_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-harding1-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> also forms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">coordination complexes</a> with transition metal ions. More than 30 such complexes have been synthesized and characterized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tramsek_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-tramsek-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas the xenon fluorides are well characterized, with the exception of dichloride <a href="/wiki/Xenon_dichloride" title="Xenon dichloride">XeCl<sub>2</sub></a>, the other halides are not known. Xenon dichloride, formed by the high-frequency irradiation of a mixture of xenon, fluorine, and <a href="/wiki/Silicon_tetrachloride" title="Silicon tetrachloride">silicon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scott1_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-scott1-94">[94]</a></sup> is reported to be an endothermic, colorless, crystalline compound that decomposes into the elements at 80 °C. However, <span class="chemf nowrap">XeCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> may be merely a <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_molecule" title="Van der Waals molecule">van der Waals molecule</a> of weakly bound Xe atoms and <span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> molecules and not a real compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Theoretical calculations indicate that the linear molecule <span class="chemf nowrap">XeCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is less stable than the van der Waals complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides_and_oxohalides">Oxides and oxohalides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Oxides and oxohalides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Three oxides of xenon are known: <a href="/wiki/Xenon_trioxide" title="Xenon trioxide">xenon trioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Xenon_tetroxide" title="Xenon tetroxide">xenon tetroxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), both of which are dangerously explosive and powerful oxidizing agents, and <a href="/wiki/Xenon_dioxide" title="Xenon dioxide">xenon dioxide</a> (XeO<sub>2</sub>), which was reported in 2011 with a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> of four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> XeO<sub>2</sub> forms when xenon tetrafluoride is poured over ice. Its crystal structure may allow it to replace silicon in silicate minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChemistryWhere2011_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChemistryWhere2011-98">[98]</a></sup> The XeOO<sup>+</sup> cation has been identified by <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">infrared spectroscopy</a> in solid <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon does not react with oxygen directly; the trioxide is formed by the hydrolysis of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 6 HF</dd></dl>
<p><span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> is weakly acidic, dissolving in alkali to form unstable <i>xenate</i> salts containing the <span class="chemf nowrap">HXeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> anion. These unstable salts easily <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionate</a> into xenon gas and <i><a href="/wiki/Perxenate" title="Perxenate">perxenate</a></i> salts, containing the <span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> anion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henderson_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-henderson-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Barium perxenate, when treated with concentrated <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, yields gaseous xenon tetroxide:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scott1_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-scott1-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Ba<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">BaSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>To prevent decomposition, the xenon tetroxide thus formed is quickly cooled to form a pale-yellow solid. It explodes above −35.9 °C into xenon and oxygen gas.
</p><p>A number of xenon oxyfluorides are known, including <span class="chemf nowrap">XeOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Xenon_oxytetrafluoride" title="Xenon oxytetrafluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">XeOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. <span class="chemf nowrap">XeOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> is formed by reacting <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_difluoride" title="Oxygen difluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">OF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></a> with xenon gas at low temperatures. It may also be obtained by partial hydrolysis of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>. It disproportionates at −20 °C into <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mackay1_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-mackay1-102">[102]</a></sup> <span class="chemf nowrap">XeOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is formed by the partial hydrolysis of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> or the reaction of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> with sodium perxenate, <span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>. The latter reaction also produces a small amount of <span class="chemf nowrap">XeO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. <span class="chemf nowrap">XeOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> reacts with <a href="/wiki/Caesium_fluoride" title="Caesium fluoride">CsF</a> to form the <span class="chemf nowrap">XeOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> anion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mackay1_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-mackay1-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> while XeOF<sub>3</sub> reacts with the alkali metal fluorides <a href="/wiki/Potassium_fluoride" title="Potassium fluoride">KF</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubidium_fluoride" title="Rubidium fluoride">RbF</a> and CsF to form the <span class="chemf nowrap">XeOF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> anion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_compounds">Other compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recently, there has been an interest in xenon compounds where xenon is directly bonded to a less electronegative element than fluorine or oxygen, particularly <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Electron-withdrawing groups, such as groups with fluorine substitution, are necessary to stabilize these compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henderson_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-henderson-101">[101]</a></sup> Numerous such compounds have been characterized, including:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mackay1_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-mackay1-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>–Xe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>–N≡C–CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, where C<sub>6</sub>F<sub>5</sub> is the pentafluorophenyl group.</li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap">[C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Xe</span></li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>–Xe–C≡N</span></li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>–Xe–F</span></li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>–Xe–Cl</span></li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>–C≡C–Xe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap">[CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>C–C≡C–Xe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></li>
