<li><span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>–XeF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></li>
<li><span class="chemf nowrap">(C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>Xe)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></li></ul>
<p>Other compounds containing xenon bonded to a less electronegative element include <span class="chemf nowrap">F–Xe–N(SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">F–Xe–BF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. The latter is synthesized from <a href="/wiki/Dioxygenyl" title="Dioxygenyl">dioxygenyl</a> tetrafluoroborate, <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>BF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, at −100 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mackay1_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-mackay1-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>An unusual ion containing xenon is the <a href="/wiki/Tetraxenonogold(II)" title="Tetraxenonogold(II)">tetraxenonogold(II)</a> cation, <span class="chemf nowrap">AuXe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, which contains Xe–Au bonds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waikeeli2_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-waikeeli2-109">[109]</a></sup> This ion occurs in the compound <span class="chemf nowrap">AuXe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(Sb<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">11</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, and is remarkable in having direct chemical bonds between two notoriously unreactive atoms, xenon and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, with xenon acting as a transition metal ligand.
</p><p>The compound <span class="chemf nowrap">Xe<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Sb<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">11</sub></span></span> contains a Xe–Xe bond, the longest element-element bond known (308.71 pm = 3.0871 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angstrom" title="Angstrom">Å</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, M. Räsänen and co-workers, scientists at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Helsinki" title="University of Helsinki">University of Helsinki</a> in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, announced the preparation of xenon dihydride (HXeH), and later xenon hydride-hydroxide (HXeOH), hydroxenoacetylene (HXeCCH), and other Xe-containing molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> In 2008, Khriachtchev <i>et al.</i> reported the preparation of HXeOXeH by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> of water within a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> xenon matrix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">Deuterated</a> molecules, HXeOD and DXeOH, have also been produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clathrates_and_excimers">Clathrates and excimers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Clathrates and excimers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to compounds where xenon forms a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bond</a>, xenon can form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate" title="Clathrate">clathrates</a>—substances where xenon atoms or pairs are trapped by the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystalline lattice</a> of another compound. One example is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenon_hydrate" title="Xenon hydrate">xenon hydrate</a> (Xe•5.75 H<sub>2</sub>O), where xenon atoms occupy vacancies in a lattice of water molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> This clathrate has a melting point of 24 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-henderson2_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-henderson2-115">[115]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuterate" title="Deuterate">deuterated</a> version of this hydrate has also been produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Another example is xenon <a href="/wiki/Hydride" title="Hydride">hydride</a> (Xe(H<sub>2</sub>)<sub>8</sub>), in which xenon pairs (dimers) are trapped inside <a href="/wiki/Solid_hydrogen" title="Solid hydrogen">solid hydrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KrH_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-KrH-117">[117]</a></sup> Such <a href="/wiki/Clathrate_hydrate" title="Clathrate hydrate">clathrate hydrates</a> can occur naturally under conditions of high pressure,
such as in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Vostok" title="Lake Vostok">Lake Vostok</a> underneath the <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctic</a> ice sheet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Clathrate formation can be used to fractionally distill xenon, argon and krypton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon can also form <a href="/wiki/Endohedral_fullerene" title="Endohedral fullerene">endohedral fullerene</a> compounds, where a xenon atom is trapped inside a <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerene</a> molecule. The xenon atom trapped in the fullerene can be observed by <sup>129</sup>Xe <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a> (NMR) spectroscopy. Through the sensitive <a href="/wiki/Chemical_shift" title="Chemical shift">chemical shift</a> of the xenon atom to its environment, chemical reactions on the fullerene molecule can be analyzed. These observations are not without caveat, however, because the xenon atom has an electronic influence on the reactivity of the fullerene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>When xenon atoms are in the <a href="/wiki/Stationary_state" title="Stationary state">ground energy state</a>, they repel each other and will not form a bond. When xenon atoms becomes energized, however, they can form an <a href="/wiki/Excimer" title="Excimer">excimer</a> (excited dimer) until the electrons return to the <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a>. This entity is formed because the xenon atom tends to complete the outermost <a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">electronic shell</a> by adding an electron from a neighboring xenon atom. The typical lifetime of a xenon excimer is 1–5 ns, and the decay releases <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> with <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of about 150 and 173 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Xenon can also form excimers with other elements, such as the <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although xenon is rare and relatively expensive to extract from the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a>, it has a number of applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Illumination_and_optics">Illumination and optics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Illumination and optics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas-discharge_lamps">Gas-discharge lamps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gas-discharge lamps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Xenon is used in light-emitting devices called xenon flash lamps, used in <a href="/wiki/Flash_(photography)" title="Flash (photography)">photographic flashes</a> and stroboscopic lamps;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burke_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-burke-14">[14]</a></sup> to excite the <a href="/wiki/Active_laser_medium" title="Active laser medium">active medium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a> which then generate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coherent_light" title="Coherent light">coherent light</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> and, occasionally, in <a href="/wiki/Bactericide" title="Bactericide">bactericidal</a> lamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The first solid-state <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a>, invented in 1960, was pumped by a xenon flash lamp,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toyserkani_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-toyserkani-18">[18]</a></sup> and lasers used to power <a href="/wiki/Inertial_confinement_fusion" title="Inertial confinement fusion">inertial confinement fusion</a> are also pumped by xenon flash lamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg"><img alt="Elongated glass sphere with two metal rod electrodes inside, facing each other. One electrode is blunt and another is sharpened." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg/220px-Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg/330px-Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg/440px-Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_short_arc_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Xenon short-arc lamp</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1996" data-file-width="3000" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg/220px-STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg/330px-STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg/440px-STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-135_Atlantis_rollout_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> bathed in xenon lights</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3008" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_discharge_tube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Xenon gas discharge tube</div></div></div>
<p>Continuous, short-arc, high pressure <a href="/wiki/Xenon_arc_lamp" title="Xenon arc lamp">xenon arc lamps</a> have a <a href="/wiki/Color_temperature" title="Color temperature">color temperature</a> closely approximating noon sunlight and are used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_Simulator" title="Solar Simulator">solar simulators</a>. That is, the <a href="/wiki/Chromaticity" title="Chromaticity">chromaticity</a> of these lamps closely approximates a heated <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> radiator at the temperature of the Sun. First introduced in the 1940s, these lamps replaced the shorter-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_arc_lamp" title="Carbon arc lamp">carbon arc lamps</a> in movie projectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mellor_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-mellor-15">[15]</a></sup> They are also employed in typical <a href="/wiki/35_mm_film" title="35 mm film">35mm</a>, <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">IMAX</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_projectors" title="Digital projectors">digital</a> <a href="/wiki/Movie_projector" title="Movie projector">film projection</a> systems. They are an excellent source of short wavelength <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation and have intense emissions in the near <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> used in some <a href="/wiki/Night_vision" title="Night vision">night vision</a> systems. Xenon is used as a starter gas in  <a href="/wiki/High-intensity_discharge_lamp" title="High-intensity discharge lamp">HID</a> automotive headlights, and high-end <a href="/wiki/Tactical_light" title="Tactical light">"tactical" flashlights</a>.
</p><p>The individual cells in a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_display" title="Plasma display">plasma display</a> contain a mixture of xenon and neon ionized with <a href="/wiki/Electrode" title="Electrode">electrodes</a>. The interaction of this plasma with the electrodes generates ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>, which then excite the <a href="/wiki/Phosphor" title="Phosphor">phosphor</a> coating on the front of the display.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon is used as a "starter gas" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_vapor_lamp" title="Sodium vapor lamp">high pressure sodium lamps</a>. It has the lowest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> and lowest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionization_potential" title="Ionization potential">ionization potential</a> of all the non-radioactive noble gases. As a noble gas, it does not interfere with the chemical reactions occurring in the operating lamp. The low thermal conductivity minimizes thermal losses in the lamp while in the operating state, and the low ionization potential causes the <a href="/wiki/Breakdown_voltage" title="Breakdown voltage">breakdown voltage</a> of the gas to be relatively low in the cold state, which allows the lamp to be more easily started.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Lasers">Lasers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Lasers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1962, a group of researchers at <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Laboratories</a> discovered laser action in xenon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> and later found that the laser gain was improved by adding <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> to the lasing medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Excimer_laser" title="Excimer laser">excimer laser</a> used a xenon <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a> (Xe<sub>2</sub>) energized by a beam of electrons to produce <a href="/wiki/Stimulated_emission" title="Stimulated emission">stimulated emission</a> at an <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> wavelength of 176 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basov_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-basov-17">[17]</a></sup>
Xenon chloride and xenon fluoride have also been used in excimer (or, more accurately, exciplex) lasers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anesthesia">Anesthesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Anesthesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Xenon has been used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthetic" title="General anesthetic">general anesthetic</a>. Although it is expensive, anesthesia machines that can deliver xenon are expected to appear on the European market because advances in recovery and recycling of xenon have made it economically viable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singh_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-singh-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon interacts with many different receptors and ion channels, and like many theoretically multi-modal inhalation anesthetics, these interactions are likely complementary. Xenon is a high-affinity glycine-site <a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor_antagonist" title="NMDA receptor antagonist">NMDA receptor antagonist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmda_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-nmda-136">[136]</a></sup> However, xenon is different from certain other NMDA receptor antagonists in that it is not <a href="/wiki/Neurotoxicity" title="Neurotoxicity">neurotoxic</a> and it inhibits the neurotoxicity of <a href="/wiki/Ketamine" title="Ketamine">ketamine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>, while actually producing <a href="/wiki/Neuroprotection" title="Neuroprotection">neuroprotective effects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Unlike ketamine and nitrous oxide, xenon does not stimulate a dopamine efflux in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">nucleus accumbens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Like nitrous oxide and <a href="/wiki/Cyclopropane" title="Cyclopropane">cyclopropane</a>, xenon activates the two-pore domain potassium channel <a href="/wiki/KCNK2" title="KCNK2">TREK-1</a>. A related channel <a href="/wiki/KCNK9" title="KCNK9">TASK-3</a> also implicated in the actions of inhalation anesthetics is insensitive to xenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Xenon inhibits nicotinic acetylcholine <a href="/wiki/Alpha-4_beta-2_nicotinic_receptor" title="Alpha-4 beta-2 nicotinic receptor">α<sub>4</sub>β<sub>2</sub></a> receptors which contribute to spinally mediated analgesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Xenon is an effective inhibitor of <a href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane_Ca2%2B_ATPase" title="Plasma membrane Ca2+ ATPase">plasma membrane Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase</a>. Xenon inhibits Ca<sup>2+</sup> ATPase by binding to a hydrophobic pore within the enzyme and preventing the enzyme from assuming active conformations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon is a competitive inhibitor of the serotonin <a href="/wiki/5-HT3_receptor" title="5-HT3 receptor">5-HT<sub>3</sub> receptor</a>. While neither anesthetic nor antinociceptive, this reduces anesthesia-emergent nausea and vomiting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon has a <a href="/wiki/Minimum_alveolar_concentration" title="Minimum alveolar concentration">minimum alveolar concentration</a> (MAC) of 72% at age 40, making it 44% more potent than N<sub>2</sub>O as an anesthetic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Thus, it can be used with oxygen in concentrations that have a lower risk of <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O), xenon is not a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> and is viewed as <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally friendly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goto2003_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goto2003-146">[146]</a></sup> Though recycled in modern systems, xenon vented to the atmosphere is only returning to its original source, without environmental impact.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroprotectant">Neuroprotectant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Neuroprotectant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Xenon induces robust <a href="/wiki/Cardioprotection" title="Cardioprotection">cardioprotection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroprotection" title="Neuroprotection">neuroprotection</a> through a variety of mechanisms. Through its influence on Ca<sup>2+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, KATP\HIF, and NMDA antagonism, xenon is neuroprotective when administered before, during and after <a href="/wiki/Ischemia" title="Ischemia">ischemic</a> insults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Xenon is a high affinity antagonist at the NMDA receptor glycine site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmda_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-nmda-136">[136]</a></sup> Xenon is cardioprotective in ischemia-reperfusion conditions by inducing <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacologic</a> non-ischemic preconditioning. Xenon is cardioprotective by activating PKC-epsilon and downstream p38-MAPK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Xenon mimics neuronal ischemic preconditioning by activating ATP sensitive potassium channels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Xenon allosterically reduces ATP mediated channel activation inhibition independently of the sulfonylurea receptor1 subunit, increasing KATP open-channel time and frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_doping">Sports doping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sports doping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Inhaling a xenon/oxygen mixture activates production of the <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factor</a> <a href="/wiki/HIF1A" title="HIF1A">HIF-1-alpha</a>, which may lead to increased production of <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">erythropoietin</a>. The latter hormone is known to increase <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cell</a> production and athletic performance. Reportedly, doping with xenon inhalation has been used in Russia since 2004 and perhaps earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> On August 31, 2014, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Anti_Doping_Agency" title="World Anti Doping Agency">World Anti Doping Agency</a> (WADA) added xenon (and <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>) to the list of prohibited substances and methods, although no reliable doping tests for these gases have yet been developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> In addition, effects of xenon on erythropoietin production in humans have not been demonstrated, so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Imaging">Imaging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Imaging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">Gamma</a> emission from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a> <sup>133</sup>Xe of xenon can be used to image the heart, lungs, and brain, for example, by means of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single_photon_emission_computed_tomography" title="Single photon emission computed tomography">single photon emission computed tomography</a>. <sup>133</sup>Xe has also been used to measure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_flow" title="Blood flow">blood flow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon, particularly hyperpolarized <sup>129</sup>Xe, is a useful contrast agent for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRI" title="MRI">magnetic resonance imaging</a> (MRI). In the gas phase, it can image cavities in a porous sample, alveoli in lungs, or the flow of gases within the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>  Because xenon is soluble both in water and in hydrophobic solvents, it can image various soft living tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The xenon chloride <a href="/wiki/Excimer_laser" title="Excimer laser">excimer laser</a> has certain dermatological uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NMR_spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: NMR spectroscopy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because of the xenon atom's large, flexible outer electron shell, the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">NMR</a> spectrum changes in response to surrounding conditions and can be used to monitor the surrounding chemical circumstances. For instance, xenon dissolved in water, xenon dissolved in hydrophobic solvent, and xenon associated with certain proteins can be distinguished by NMR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hyperpolarized xenon can be used by <a href="/wiki/Surface_science" title="Surface science">surface chemists</a>. Normally, it is difficult to characterize surfaces with NMR because signals from a surface are overwhelmed by signals from the atomic nuclei in the bulk of the sample, which are much more numerous than surface nuclei. However, nuclear spins on solid surfaces can be selectively polarized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_Enhanced_Nuclear_Induction_Spectroscopy" title="Proton Enhanced Nuclear Induction Spectroscopy">transferring spin polarization to them</a> from hyperpolarized xenon gas. This makes the surface signals strong enough to measure and distinguish from bulk signals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">nuclear energy</a> studies, xenon is used in <a href="/wiki/Bubble_chamber" title="Bubble chamber">bubble chambers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> probes, and in other areas where a high <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mass" title="Molecular mass">molecular weight</a> and inert chemistry is desirable. A by-product of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a> testing is the release of radioactive <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_xenon" title="Isotopes of xenon">xenon-133 and xenon-135</a>. These isotopes are monitored to ensure compliance with nuclear <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Test_Ban_Treaty_(disambiguation)" title="Test Ban Treaty (disambiguation)">test ban treaties</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> and to confirm nuclear tests by states such as <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png"><img alt="A metal cylinder with electrodes attached to its side. Blue diffuse light is coming out of the tube." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1735" data-file-width="1873" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png/220px-Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png/330px-Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png/440px-Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xenon_ion_engine_prototype.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A prototype of a xenon ion engine being tested at NASA's <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Liquid xenon is used in <a href="/wiki/Calorimeter_(particle_physics)" title="Calorimeter (particle physics)">calorimeters</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> to measure <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, and as a detector of hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">weakly interacting massive particles</a>, or WIMPs. When a WIMP collides with a xenon nucleus, theory predicts it will impart enough energy to cause ionization and <a href="/wiki/Scintillation_(physics)" title="Scintillation (physics)">scintillation</a>. Liquid xenon is useful for these experiments because its density makes dark matter interaction more likely and it permits a quiet detector through self-shielding.
</p><p>Xenon is the preferred <a href="/wiki/Propellant" title="Propellant">propellant</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_propulsion" title="Ion propulsion">ion propulsion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> because it has low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionization_potential" title="Ionization potential">ionization potential</a> per <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic weight</a> and can be stored as a liquid at near <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> (under high pressure), yet easily evaporated to feed the engine. Xenon is inert, environmentally friendly, and less corrosive to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engine</a> than other fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> or <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a>. Xenon was first used for satellite ion engines during the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> It was later employed as a propellant for JPL's <a href="/wiki/Deep_Space_1" title="Deep Space 1">Deep Space 1</a> probe, Europe's <a href="/wiki/SMART-1" title="SMART-1">SMART-1</a> spacecraft<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saccoccia_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-saccoccia-20">[20]</a></sup> and for the three ion propulsion engines on NASA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dawn_Spacecraft" title="Dawn Spacecraft">Dawn Spacecraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chemically, the <a href="/wiki/Perxenate" title="Perxenate">perxenate</a> compounds are used as <a href="/wiki/Oxidizing_agent" title="Oxidizing agent">oxidizing agents</a> in <a href="/wiki/Analytical_chemistry" title="Analytical chemistry">analytical chemistry</a>. <a href="/wiki/Xenon_difluoride" title="Xenon difluoride">Xenon difluoride</a> is used as an etchant for <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, particularly in the production of <a href="/wiki/Microelectromechanical_systems" title="Microelectromechanical systems">microelectromechanical systems</a> (MEMS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> The anticancer drug <a href="/wiki/Fluorouracil" title="Fluorouracil">5-fluorouracil</a> can be produced by reacting xenon difluoride with <a href="/wiki/Uracil" title="Uracil">uracil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Xenon is also used in <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">protein crystallography</a>. Applied at pressures from 0.5 to 5 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a> (5 to 50 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atm</a>) to a protein crystal, xenon atoms bind in predominantly <a href="/wiki/Hydrophobe" title="Hydrophobe">hydrophobic</a> cavities, often creating a high-quality, isomorphous, heavy-atom derivative that can be used for solving the <a href="/wiki/Phase_problem" title="Phase problem">phase problem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Xenon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox bordered" style="border-collapse:collapse; width:22em; text-align:left; ;">
<caption style="text-align:center;">Xenon
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="2" style="background: #f8eaba; text-align: center;">Hazards
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/NFPA_704" title="NFPA 704">NFPA 704</a>
</td>
<td><div style="width:100%; background:transparent;"><div id="container" style="margin:0 auto; width:82px; font-family:sans-serif"><div class="nounderlines" id="on_image_elements" style="background:; float:left; font-size:20px; text-align:center; vertical-align:middle; position:relative; height:80px; width:80px; padding:1px;">
<div id="diamond_image_and_mw_ImageMap" style="position:absolute; height:80px; width:80px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_1_529938878"><area alt="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., liquid nitrogen"/><area alt="Special hazards (white): no code" coords="40,40,60,60,40,80,20,60" href="/wiki/NFPA_704#White" shape="poly" title="Special hazards (white): no code"/></map><img alt="NFPA 704 four-colored diamond" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/80px-NFPA_704.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/120px-NFPA_704.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/NFPA_704.svg/160px-NFPA_704.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_529938878" width="80"/></div></div><div style="width:12px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:12px; left:35px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 0: Exposure under fire conditions would offer no hazard beyond that of ordinary combustible material. E.g., sodium chloride">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Because they are strongly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidative" title="Oxidative">oxidative</a>, many oxygen-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenon_compounds" title="Xenon compounds">xenon compounds</a> are toxic; they are also explosive (highly <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_process" title="Exothermic process">exothermic</a>), breaking down to elemental xenon and <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic</a> oxygen (O<sub>2</sub>) with much stronger chemical bonds than the xenon compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finkel68_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-finkel68-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>Xenon gas can be safely kept in normal sealed glass or metal containers at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>. However, it readily dissolves in most plastics and rubber, and will gradually escape from a container sealed with such materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Xenon is non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxic</a>, although it does dissolve in blood and belongs to a select group of substances that penetrate the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>, causing mild to full surgical <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthesia</a> when inhaled in high concentrations with oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finkel68_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-finkel68-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a> in xenon gas (169 m/s) is less than that in air<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> because the average velocity of the heavy xenon atoms is less than that of nitrogen and oxygen molecules in air. Hence, xenon vibrates more slowly in the <a href="/wiki/Vocal_tract" title="Vocal tract">vocal cords</a> when exhaled and produces lowered voice tones, an effect opposite to the high-toned voice produced in <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. Like helium, xenon does not satisfy the body's need for oxygen, and it is both a simple <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant_gas" title="Asphyxiant gas">asphyxiant</a> and an anesthetic more powerful than nitrous oxide; consequently, and because xenon is expensive, many universities have prohibited the voice stunt as a general chemistry demonstration. The gas <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">sulfur hexafluoride</a> is similar to xenon in molecular weight (146 versus 131), less expensive, and though an asphyxiant, not toxic or anesthetic; it is often substituted in these demonstrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dense gases such as xenon and sulfur hexafluoride can be breathed safely when mixed with at least 20% oxygen. Xenon at 80% concentration along with 20% oxygen rapidly produces the unconsciousness of general anesthesia (and has been used for this, as discussed above). Breathing mixes gases of different densities very effectively and rapidly so that heavier gases are purged along with the oxygen, and do not accumulate at the bottom of the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> There is, however, a danger associated with any heavy gas in large quantities: it may sit invisibly in a container, and a person who enters an area filled with an odorless, colorless gas may be asphyxiated without warning. Xenon is rarely used in large enough quantities for this to be a concern, though the potential for danger exists any time a tank or container of xenon is kept in an unventilated space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
